                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Lamiaceae, the mint family of flowering plants, has broad distribution and, with more than 7000 species and it is the largest family of the order Lamiales. Plants from this family are valued for their flavor, fragrance and medicinal properties. <i>Pogostemon</i> is a large genus from this family and one of the best-known members of this genus is patchouli, <i>Pogostemon cablin</i>. Patchouli is an annual herb native to the Philippines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Arpana, J., Bagyaraj, D., Prakasa Rao, E., Parameswaran, T. &amp; Abdul Rahiman, B. Symbiotic response of patchouli [Pogostemon cablin (Blanco) Benth.] to different arbuscular mycorrhizal fungi. Adv. Environ. Biol. 2, 20â24 (2008)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d51722e483">1</a></sup> and has been widely cultivated in tropical and subtropical areas of Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Wu, Y.-G. et al. Genetic diversity analysis among and within populations of Pogostemon cablin from China with ISSR and SRAP markers. Biochem. Syst. Ecol. 38, 63â72 (2010)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d51722e487">2</a></sup> owing to high demand for its essential oil and precious material for traditional medicine in China. The patchouli alcohol, which is abundant in the plant leaves, is an important ingredient for perfumes, incense, soaps and cosmetic products<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Leung, A. Y. &amp; Foster, S. Encyclopedia of common natural ingredients used in food, drugs and cosmetics. (John Wiley &amp; Sons, Inc., 1996)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d51722e491">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Bauer, K., Garbe, D. &amp; Surburg, H. Common fragrance and flavor materials: preparation, properties and uses. (John Wiley &amp; Sons, 2008)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d51722e494">4</a></sup>. Patchouli is also an essential Chinese medicinal plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chen, M. et al. Analysis of Pogostemon cablin from pharmaceutical research to market performances. Expert Opin. Inv. Drug. 22, 245â257 (2013)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d51722e499">5</a></sup> recorded in Chinese Pharmacopoeia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Committee, N. P. Chinese pharmacopoeia. 70â71 (China Medical Science Press, 2010)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d51722e503">6</a></sup> for its therapeutic roles in heat and dampness elimination, nerve smoothness and fatigue alleviation. Patchouli is also a component of some proprietary Chinese medicines that could be used to treat indigestion, headache and fever<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Committee, N. P. Chinese pharmacopoeia. 70â71 (China Medical Science Press, 2010)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d51722e507">6</a></sup>.</p><p>Chemical and pharmacological studies of <i>P. cablin</i> over the last few decades indicate that patchouli contains &gt;40 major components, including mono- and sesqui-terpenoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kiuchi, F., Matsuo, K., Ito, M., Qui, T. K. &amp; Honda, G. New sesquiterpene hydroperoxides with trypanocidal activity from Pogostemon cablin. Chem. Pharm. Bulletin 52, 1495â1496 (2004)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d51722e517">7</a></sup>, triterpenoids and steroids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Guan, L., Quan, L., Xu, L. &amp; Cong, P. [Chemical constituents of Pogostemon cablin (Blanco) Benth.]. Zhongguo Zhong yao za zhi= Zhongguo zhongyao zazhi= China journal of Chinese materia medica 19, 355â356, 383 (1994)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d51722e521">8</a></sup>, flavonoids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Miyazawa, M., Okuno, Y., Nakamura, S.-i. &amp; Kosaka, H. Antimutagenic activity of flavonoids from Pogostemon cablin. J. Agr. Food Chem. 48, 642â647 (2000)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d51722e525">9</a></sup> and alkaloids and phenylpropanoid glycosides<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Li, P. et al. Simultaneous determination of eight flavonoids and pogostone in Pogostemon cablin by high performance liquid chromatography. J. Liq. Chromatog. R. T. 37, 1771â1784 (2014)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d51722e529">10</a></sup>. The constituents exhibit marked activities, such as antibacterial<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Hussain, A. I. et al. Antibacterial activity of some Lamiaceae essential oils using resazurin as an indicator of cell growth. LWT-Food Sci. Technol. 44, 1199â1206 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d51722e534">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kongkathip, N. et al. Development of patchouli extraction with quality control and isolation of active compounds with antibacterial activity. Kasetsart J. (Nat. Sci.) 43, 519 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d51722e537">12</a></sup>, anti-influenza virus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Kiyohara, H. et al. Patchouli alcohol: in vitro direct anti-influenza virus sesquiterpene in Pogostemon cablin Benth. J. Nat. Med. 66, 55â61 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d51722e541">13</a></sup>, anti-inflammatory<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Li, Y.-C. et al. Anti-inflammatory activity of patchouli alcohol isolated from Pogostemonis Herba in animal models. Fitoterapia 82, 1295â1301 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d51722e545">14</a></sup>, cytotoxic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Park, E. J., Park, H. R., Lee, J. S. &amp; Kim, J. Licochalcone A: an inducer of cell differentiation and cytotoxic agent from Pogostemon cablin. Planta Med. 64, 464â466 (1998)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d51722e549">15</a></sup>, antimutagenic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Miyazawa, M., Okuno, Y., Nakamura, S.-i. &amp; Kosaka, H. Antimutagenic activity of flavonoids from Pogostemon cablin. J. Agr. Food Chem. 48, 642â647 (2000)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d51722e553">9</a></sup>, anti-PAFâinduced platelet aggregation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tsai, Y.-C. et al. Î±-Bulnesene, a PAF inhibitor isolated from the essential oil of Pogostemon cablin. Fitoterapia 78, 7â11 (2007)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d51722e557">16</a></sup>, insecticidal and hepatoprotective activities<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, F. et al. Four new sesquiterpenes from the stems of Pogostemon cablin. Fitoterapia 86, 183â187 (2013)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d51722e562">17</a></sup>. Among these components, sesquiterpenoids and flavonoids are most abundant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zeng, S., Ouyang, P., Mo, X. &amp; Wang, Y. Characterization of genes coding phenylalanine ammonia lyase and chalcone synthase in four Pogostemon cablin cultivars. Biol. Plantarum 59, 298â304 (2015)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d51722e566">18</a></sup>. However, although some genes involved in terpenoid and flavonoid biosynthesis have been isolated and functionally characterized<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Zeng, S., Ouyang, P., Mo, X. &amp; Wang, Y. Characterization of genes coding phenylalanine ammonia lyase and chalcone synthase in four Pogostemon cablin cultivars. Biol. Plantarum 59, 298â304 (2015)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d51722e570">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Deguerry, F. et al. The diverse sesquiterpene profile of patchouli, Pogostemon cablin, is correlated with a limited number of sesquiterpene synthases. Arch. Biochem. Biophys. 454, 123â136 (2006)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d51722e573">19</a></sup>, the global terpenoid and flavonoid biosynthesis pathways in <i>P. cablin</i> remain to be fully characterized.</p><p>In this genomic era, deciphering genomes of medicinal plants is of great importance in understanding and improving these poorly investigated species and will enable insights into the biochemistry and evolution of genes responsible for secondary metabolism biosynthetic pathways. Recent advances in genome sequencing technologies and assembly algorithms have resulted in the generation of genome sequences for a wide range of plant species. However, very few studies have examined the genomes of medicinal plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Chen, S., Xiang, L., Guo, X. &amp; Li, Q. An introduction to the medicinal plant genome project. Front. Med. 5, 178â184, 10.1007/s11684-011-0131-0 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d51722e583">20</a></sup>. Currently, only the genomes of two medicinal mushrooms (<i>Ganoderma lucidum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Chen, S. et al. Genome sequence of the model medicinal mushroom Ganoderma lucidum. Nat. Commun. 3, 913, 10.1038/ncomms1923 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d51722e589">21</a></sup> and <i>G. sinense</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Zhu, Y. et al. Chromosome-level genome map provides insights into diverse defense mechanisms in the medicinal fungus Ganoderma sinense. Sci. Rep. 5, 11087, 10.1038/srep11087 (2015)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d51722e595">22</a></sup>) and two medicinal flowering plants (<i>Dendrobium officinale</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Yan, L. et al. The Genome of Dendrobium officinale Illuminates the Biology of the Important Traditional Chinese Orchid Herb. Mol. Plant 8, 922â934, 10.1016/j.molp.2014.12.011 (2015)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d51722e601">23</a></sup> and <i>Catharanthus roseus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kellner, F. et al. Genome-guided investigation of plant natural product biosynthesis. Plant J. 82, 680â692 (2015)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d51722e608">24</a></sup>) have been reported and these studies highlight that genomic data could play an important role in investigating secondary metabolic pathways.</p><p>Despite the prominent roles of patchouli in both industrial and traditional Chinese medicine, our understanding of its biology is limited by a lack of genomic resources. To set up the foundation for genomic studies of patchouli, we assembled the draft genome of <i>P. cablin</i>, which is also the first draft genome of Lamiaceae. Although cost and time restraints prohibited assembly of the complete genome, this draft genome does capture the vast majority of patchouli genomic regions. The availability of this genome sequence not only is an invaluable resource for elucidating evolutionary processes in the Lamiaceae lineage but also provides insight into the underlying molecular mechanism of the sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis pathway in patchouli. This genomic resource will serve as a foundation for further investigation of patchouli biology and for the selection of cultivars with improved medicinal and pharmaceutical traits.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results and Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and quality control</h3><p>To obtain sufficient quantities of nuclear DNA for the sequencing libraries, nuclear DNA was isolated from leaves of <i>P. cablin</i>. DNA libraries with insert sizes of 180âbp and 500âbp were sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform, generating ~52.62âGb and 10.88âGb, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S1</a>). A total of &gt;43âGb of clean bases were obtained after removing PCR duplicates, low-quality reads, adaptor sequences and contaminating reads (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S1</a>).</p><p>Although organelle genomes are considerably smaller than the nuclear genome, incorrect nuclear DNA extraction can lead to a high percentage of organelle DNA in sequencing libraries, which can dramatically decrease the validity of the data and hinder genome assembly. Therefore, clean reads were aligned to organelle sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S2</a>) using SOAP2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: an improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966â1967 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d51722e645">25</a></sup>. Only 2.91% of the reads aligned (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S3</a>), indicating a low percentage of organelle DNA in our samples. Contamination from microorganisms or other species can also decrease the proportion of valid data. To determine whether there were sequences from other species in the clean data, 10,000 clean reads were randomly selected to align to the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) nucleotide (NT) database. Most reads (98.36%) aligned successfully to a particular species and no significant bias for a plant species and the only significant bias for non-plant species was <i>Dictyostelium discoideum</i> (4.74%, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S4</a>), indicating that DNA contamination was inconspicuous.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genomic characteristics</h3><p>Prior to <i>de novo</i> assembly of the genome, we were able to obtain some key genomic features, such as GC content, genome size and repeat content and heterozygosity rates. These characteristics are vital prior knowledge to guide the sequencing approach and to optimize parameters for the assembly process. Examining the distribution of distinct K-mers is one way to inspect such features. As shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig1">Fig. 1A</a>, the main peak was at a sequencing depth of 19 and there was no small peak at the depth of 9â10, indicating that the heterozygosity rate of patchouli is relatively low. However, the distribution displays a fat tail, which indicates that this genome contains a high proportion of repeats (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig1">Fig. 1A</a>). Indeed, quantitative results from the ALLPATH-LG assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d51722e675">26</a></sup> also support these conclusions. The patchouli genome was estimated to be ~1576âMb and the estimated percentage of repetitive sequences was as high as 72.6%, which is expected when considering the large genome size. The estimated heterozygosity of the reads was 0.46%, a medium level in plants.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Figure 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="628" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Distribution pattern of 21-mer, sequencing depth, heterozygosity rate and biallelic SNPs.</p><p>(<b>A</b>) 21-mer distribution. The <i>y</i>-axis represents the frequency at a given depth divided by the total frequency of all depths. (<b>B</b>) Sequencing depth distribution. The <i>y</i>-axis is the proportion of the base number at each sequencing depth divided by the total sequenced bases. (<b>C</b>) Heterozygous SNP density distribution. Heterozygous SNPs from patchouli diploid genomic data were identified. Non-overlapping 10-kb windows were chosen and the heterozygosity density was calculated. (<b>D</b>) Biallelic SNP distribution. Blue, transition; brown, transversion.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assembly</h3><p>The clean reads were <i>de novo</i> assembled using the de Bruijn graph-based SOAPdenovo2 assembler. Multiply K-mer sizes, including 33, 43, 53 and 63, were examined using default parameters. To obtain the highest number of complete genes, the assembled sequences generated with Kâ=â63 were chosen for the following analyses, as the N50 length was longer than those from other K-mer sizes. The resultant scaffolds were then subjected to gap-filling with the Illumina paired-end (PE) reads by GapCloser, scaffolds shorter than 200 bases or matched to microorganism genomes were removed and the remaining scaffolds were designated as PCAB_r1.0.</p><p>PCAB_r1.0 contains 1,608,748 scaffolds of 1150âMb total, with contig and scaffold N50 sizes of 416âbp and 1112âbp, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Approximately 50% of the total sequence was covered by 238,223 scaffolds of &gt;1112âbp, with the largest scaffold spanning 73âkb. Our assembly is ~73% of the predicted genome size (1576âMb). This is expected because of the abundant repetitive sequences in the patchouli genome, as it has been demonstrated that assembled genome size can be inflated when there is high repeat content and/or heterozygosity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tenaillon, M. I., Hufford, M. B., Gaut, B. S. &amp; Ross-Ibarra, J. Genome size and transposable element content as determined by high-throughput sequencing in maize and Zea luxurians. Genome Biol. Evol. 3, 219â229 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d51722e735">27</a></sup>. This percentage is quite similar in the Lamiales relative sesame (<i>Sesamum indicum</i> L., 274âMb relative to an estimated 357âMb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome sequencing of the high oil crop sesame provides insight into oil biosynthesis. Genome Biol. 15, R39 (2014)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51722e742">28</a></sup>, although the assembled patchouli genome is much larger.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the patchouli genome assembly PCAB_r1.0.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep26405/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To assess the quality of the assembly, we aligned all the genome reads to the assembly and then homozygous single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) were called. Approximately 75.28% of reads were mapped, suggesting a high degree of representation of the patchouli genome in our assembly. Analysis of single base-sequencing coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig1">Fig. 1B</a>) revealed a median sequencing depth of 21 and that &gt;95% of the assembly had a coverage &gt;5. The estimated sequencing error or potential misassembly rate was &lt;4âbp per 100âkb, as reflected by homozygous SNPs.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Heterozygosity rate</h3><p>Analysis of whole-genome sequences can provide a general overview of the total genetic variation in a species. In this study we identified 3,147,333 heterozygous SNPs and 490,407 insertions and deletions (InDels, length â¤10âbp) in the patchouli genome. This gave an estimated heterozygosity rate (mean per-nucleotide heterozygosity) of 0.274%, revealing relatively high nucleotide diversity. The distributions of SNP density in non-overlapping 10âkb windows revealed a single peak (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig1">Fig. 1C</a>), in which the SNP density is similar to the estimated heterozygosity rate. Among these ~3.2 million allelic sites, only 1267 SNPs are multi-allelic. The distribution of types of biallelic sites (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig1">Fig. 1D</a>) revealed a high percentage of purine-purine and pyridine-pyridine types. This is not unexpected considering the higher probability of transition than transversion.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Repeat analysis</h3><p>Transposable elements (TEs) are a major component of plant genomes and play an important role in plant genome evolution. Comprehensive annotation showed that repeat elements in patchouli make up &gt;673âMb (58.55% of the assembly; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab2">Table 2</a>). This percentage is lower than that estimated by ALLPATH-LG (72.6%). However, these two estimations are quite similar when considering that &gt;461âMb of the unassembled sequences are generally highly repetitive. The high proportion of repeats is consistent with the fat tail in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig1">Fig. 1A</a> and explains, at least in part, the large genome size of patchouli. When compared with closely related species, the repeat content of patchouli is higher than that of sesame (28.5%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome sequencing of the high oil crop sesame provides insight into oil biosynthesis. Genome Biol. 15, R39 (2014)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51722e937">28</a></sup>, grapevine (52.2%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467, 10.1038/nature06148 (2007)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d51722e941">29</a></sup> and potato (54.5%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Potato Genome Sequencing, C. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195, 10.1038/nature10158 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d51722e945">30</a></sup> but lower than that of tomato (63.2%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tomato Genome, C. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641, 10.1038/nature11119 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d51722e950">31</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab2">Table 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S5</a>). As observed in other sequenced genomes, long terminal repeats (LTRs) make up the majority (22.63%) of repeat sequences in patchouli. A high percentage of unclassified TEs (22.40%, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab2">Table 2</a>) indicated a high percentage of patchouli-specific TE categories. For the two major classes of LTRs, the proportion of Copia in patchouli (7.13%) is comparable to that in sesame (7.3%), grapevine (5.16%) and tomato (6.3%) but higher than that in potato (3.8%), whereas the percentage of Gypsy (14.95%) is comparable to that in potato (15.2%) and tomato (19.7%) and much higher than that in sesame (6.6%) and grapevine (3.7%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome sequencing of the high oil crop sesame provides insight into oil biosynthesis. Genome Biol. 15, R39 (2014)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51722e963">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467, 10.1038/nature06148 (2007)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d51722e966">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Potato Genome Sequencing, C. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195, 10.1038/nature10158 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d51722e969">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tomato Genome, C. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641, 10.1038/nature11119 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d51722e972">31</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Statistics of the repetitive sequences in the patchouli genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep26405/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Two older TE bursts were identified in the patchouli genome (genetic distances of ~0.10â0.14 and ~0.20â0.24 from the consensus; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig2">Fig. 2A</a>) and no recent bursts were observed, indicating the accumulation of many older LTRs and many fewer recent LTRs. These distributions reflect a steady-state stochastic birth/death model for the dynamics of LTR accumulation and activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Potato Genome Sequencing, C. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195, 10.1038/nature10158 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1480">30</a></sup>. Unlike what has been observed in sesame, the divergence of Gypsy in patchouli shows a normal distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig2">Fig. 2A</a>), indicating that this species had experienced explosive accumulation or activity, similar to what has been found in tomato and potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome sequencing of the high oil crop sesame provides insight into oil biosynthesis. Genome Biol. 15, R39 (2014)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1487">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Figure 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="580" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Genome annotation.</p><p>(<b>A</b>) Divergence distribution of TEs in the patchouli genome. (<b>B</b>) Distribution of CDS length. The axis are log-10 transferred. (<b>C</b>) Distribution of intron length. The axis are log-10 transferred. (<b>D</b>) GO annotations of patchouli genes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genes in patchouli</h3><p>A total of 45,020 genes (average length, 431 bases; average GC content, 51.2%) were predicted in PCAB_r1.0 by Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435âW439 (2006)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1531">32</a></sup> with a training set from <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. Among the predicted genes, 25,801 (average length, 494 bases; average GC content, 51.8%) were predicted to be multi-exon genes. To avoid potential contamination by TE-related proteins, the predicted patchouli proteome was compared to the protein (BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421, 10.1186/1471-2105-10-421 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1538">33</a></sup>) and translated nucleotide (TBLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421, 10.1186/1471-2105-10-421 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1542">33</a></sup>) sequences in RepBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1546">34</a></sup>. Nearly all of the predicted genes were found to be intrinsic and only 33 were TE-related genes, suggesting efficient identification of TEs.</p><p>The number of predicted protein-coding genes in patchouli is much higher than in its Lamiaceae relative sesame (27,148)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome sequencing of the high oil crop sesame provides insight into oil biosynthesis. Genome Biol. 15, R39 (2014)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1553">28</a></sup> and Lamiales relative monkeyflowers (26,718) (<a href="http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/">http://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/</a>). Considering the fragmental character of our genome assembly and low average length of genes, the gene number in patchouli may be overestimated. Improving the sequencing depth and assembly quality of the patchouli genome and constructing a prediction model based on full-length cDNA sequences obtained from RNA sequencing will improve the quality of gene annotation. Nevertheless, with this assembly we could explore patchouli genes at the exon and intron level (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig2">Fig. 2B,C</a>). The coding sequences (CDS) of patchouli exons are longer than those from another three asterid species and from <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig2">Fig. 2B</a>), whereas the distribution pattern of introns is similar to that of other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig2">Fig. 2C</a>).</p><p>Among the predicted patchouli genes, 25,842 (57.50%) were annotated in functional databases (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab3">Table 3</a>), including the NCBI non-redundant (NR) and Clusters of Orthologous Groups (COG) databases and the Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG) and Swiss-Prot databases. As shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab3">Table 3</a>, the NR database has the highest annotation rate (25,274; 56.14%), whereas the KEGG has the lowest (4820; 10.71%). Among the predicted patchouli genes, 17,726 showed similarities to protein-encoding sequences in the Swiss-Prot database. In parallel, a total of 6513 putative genes were classified into COG functional categories. The protein-coding genes were also classified based on gene ontology (GO) by GO-Slim analysis in Blast2GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674â3676 (2005)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1586">35</a></sup> with the NR hits, from which 14,949 predicted genes were assigned to at least one GO term (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab3">Table 3</a>). The number of genes classified into each of the molecular function, cellular component and biological process categories are summarized in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig2">Fig. 2D</a>. The most abundant categories in molecular function were âcatalytic activityâ and âbindingâ, whereas âmetabolic processâ and âcellular processâ were the most abundant categories in biological process (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig2">Fig. 2D</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Functional annotation of genes from the patchouli genome.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep26405/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gene families identification</h3><p>Predicted protein sequences of patchouli and the complete protein sequence sets of another four plant species, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>S. indicum</i> and <i>Mimulus guttatus</i>, were binned into 22,106 gene families by OrthoMCL v2.0.9<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Fischer, S. et al. Using OrthoMCL to Assign Proteins to OrthoMCLâDB Groups or to Cluster Proteomes Into New Ortholog Groups. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics, 6.12. 11-16.12. 19 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d51722e1774">36</a></sup> following selfâselfâcomparisons with the BLASTP program (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab4">Table 4</a>). The number of genes in common gene families (in which all five species are represented) from patchouli is smaller than in sesame, monkey flower and tomato (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab4">Table 4</a>). However, the average number of genes per gene family of patchouli is comparable to the other species and the number of unique gene families and the number of genes in unique gene families of patchouli are much larger (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Tab4">Table 4</a>). These results underscore that the patchouli gene set is incomplete and fragmented, as we could not get all of the full-length genes from a survey genome. A total of 4,243 gene families are shared by all the five species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig3">Fig. 3A,B</a>) and within these gene families, 923 genes are single copy, meaning that only one orthologous gene exists in each gene family in each species, making them suitable for phylogenetic inference and divergence time estimation.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab4" data-test="table-caption">Table 4 Summary of the gene families among five related plant species.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/srep26405/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Figure 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="586" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Genome evolution.</p><p>(<b>A</b>) Venn diagram of gene families from five related plant species. (<b>B</b>) Spinogram depicting the composition of different categories of gene families. (<b>C</b>) Divergence time estimation. The node bars indicate 95% posterior probability intervals. The red dots correspond to calibration points and the specific calibration time is indicated in the Methods. Plioc, Pliocene; Q, Quaternary.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Phylogeny inference and divergence time estimation</h3><p>To maximize the information content of our sequences, multiple protein and corresponding CDS alignments of the 923 single-copy gene families retrieved from OrthoMCL were concatenated into a single supergene for each species using a custom Perl script. The CDS alignments were then subjected to phylogenetic analyses with Bayesian inference implemented in MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ronquist, F. &amp; Huelsenbeck, J. P. MrBayes 3: Bayesian phylogenetic inference under mixed models. Bioinformatics 19, 1572â1574 (2003)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2105">37</a></sup> using the GTR+Î+Î substitution model. The Bayesian phylogenies obtained were consistent with the maximum likelihood tree and the well-recognized Angiosperm Phylogeny Group III classification system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="The Angiosperm Phylogeny, G. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG III. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. 161, 105â121 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2109">38</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig3">Fig. 3C</a>).</p><p>Considering that the evolutionary rates are vastly different at the different codon positions, the three codon positions of the supergene concatenated from the 923 single-copy gene families were treated as three different partitions. In the combination analysis of multi-partitions by the MCMCTree program in the PAML4.7 package, the substitution model was used, but different parameters were assigned and estimated for each partition. Moreover, because evolutionary rates have also been observed to vary among species, the clock model with independent rates among lineages specified by a log-normal probability distribution was adopted.</p><p>All of the times on nodes in the divergence tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig3">Fig. 3C</a>) were well-matched to data deposited in TIMETREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Hedges, S. B., Dudley, J. &amp; Kumar, S. TimeTree: a public knowledge-base of divergence times among organisms. Bioinformatics 22, 2971â2972 (2006)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2124">39</a></sup>, a public knowledge-base of divergence times among organisms, demonstrating the high reliability of this molecular clock dating strategy. As shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig3">Fig. 3C</a>, the divergence between patchouli and sesame appears to have occurred ~52.42 (30.90â74.10) Mya, during the Eocene. The Phrymaceae (monkey flowers) and Lamiaceae-Pedaliaceae (patchouli-sesame) shared a most recent common ancestor during the Paleocene, ~62.80 (40.1â85.6) Mya. A previous study revealed a recent whole-genome duplication (WGD) event in sesame<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome sequencing of the high oil crop sesame provides insight into oil biosynthesis. Genome Biol. 15, R39 (2014)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2131">28</a></sup> that occurred independently of a parallel triplication event in the tomatoâpotato lineage during the same period. The whole-genome triplication that occurred in tomato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Tomato Genome, C. The tomato genome sequence provides insights into fleshy fruit evolution. Nature 485, 635â641, 10.1038/nature11119 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2135">31</a></sup> and was also evident in potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Potato Genome Sequencing, C. et al. Genome sequence and analysis of the tuber crop potato. Nature 475, 189â195, 10.1038/nature10158 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2140">30</a></sup> was estimated at 71 (Â±19.4) Mya based on the Ks of paralogous genes, which is earlier than the splitting of patchouli and sesame (~52âMya), therefore implying that both patchouli and sesame experienced the recent WGD. The complete patchouli genome sequence and additional high-quality Lamiaceae genomes will help to validate the phylogenetic dating of the recent WGD events that occurred before the divergence of Lamiaceae species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genes involved in sesquiterpenes biosynthesis</h3><p>To elucidate sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis in patchouli, the sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis pathwayârelated data were first extracted from the sesquiterpenoid and triterpenoid biosynthesis reference pathway (map00909) in KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M. &amp; Tanabe, M. KEGG for integration and interpretation of large-scale molecular data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D109âD114 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2152">40</a></sup>. The resultant sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis reference pathway contains 43 unique KEGG orthology (KO) entries (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S6</a>). Protein-coding genes in patchouli were then mapped onto the KEGG metabolic pathways using the KEGG automated annotation server (KAAS)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M. &amp; Tanabe, M. KEGG for integration and interpretation of large-scale molecular data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D109âD114 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2159">40</a></sup> along with the genes from several other plant species, including Arabidopsis, tomato, sesame and monkey flower. Finally, genes annotated with KO entries were mapped onto the sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis reference pathway (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S7</a>). Of the 43 KO entries in the pathway, patchouli contained the most (seven KO entries), whereas monkey flower, sesame and Arabidopsis overlapped with four KO entries each and tomato covered only three (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>). As shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>, genes in Arabidopsis are biased toward acyclic sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis, whereas genes in patchouli are involved in the biosynthesis of nearly all types of sesquiterpenoids. In addition to the coverage of the reference pathway, the gene copy numbers in patchouli were also higher than those in other species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#Fig4">Fig. 4</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Figure 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fsrep26405/MediaObjects/41598_2016_Article_BFsrep26405_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1134" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis pathway.</p><p>Circles indicate chemical components; rectangles are enzymes, with the EC numbers given; bar plots show the gene copy number in each species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/srep26405/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Plant material and DNA extraction</h3><p>Samples were collected from Fenglai Village, Yangchun City, Guangdong Province, China. High-quality genomic DNA was extracted from the leaves of <i>P. cablin</i> using an improved CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 10.1038/ncomms3640 (2013)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2211">41</a></sup>. The modified CTAB extraction buffer included 0.1âM Tris-HCl, 0.02âM EDTA, 1.4âM NaCl, 3% (w/v) CTAB and 5% (w/v) PVP K40 and -mercaptoethanol was added to the CTAB extraction buffer to ensure DNA integrity and quality. RNase A and proteinase K were used to remove RNA and protein contamination, respectively.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome sequencing and data filtering</h3><p>Two PE Illumina genomic DNA libraries (insert sizes of 180 and 500âbp, respectively) were then constructed. The libraries were sequenced (100âbp PE) on an Illumina HiSeq 2500 system. All raw sequencing files have been submitted to the NCBI Sequence Read Archive under accession number PRJNA295004. FASTQ files from all sequencing runs were imported and subjected to quality control. Clean reads were obtained by removing PCR duplicates, low-quality reads, adaptor sequences and contaminating reads of bacterial or viral origin. Additionally, sequence errors were corrected based on the K-mer frequency.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Quality assessment of sequencing data</h3><p>To determine whether the data were contaminated by other species, we randomly selected 10,000 clean reads from the library with insert size of 180âbp and these reads were aligned to the NT library in the NCBI database using the BLAST software (parameters: <i>-b 100 -v 100 -p blastn -e 1e-05 -F F</i>). We determined the species of the highest scoring BLAST hit for each read. To evaluate the percentage of contaminating reads from organelle genomes in the data, Illumina PE reads were aligned to the organelle sequences using SOAP2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: an improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966â1967 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2233">25</a></sup> (parameters: <i>-m 100 -x 280 -p 10</i>). Organelle sequences included sequences from chloroplasts (<i>P. cablin</i>, <i>Salvia miltiorrhiza</i>, <i>S. indicum</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>) and mitochondria (<i>S. miltiorrhiza</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/srep26405#MOESM2">Supplementary Table S3</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genomic characteristics, assembly, assessment and heterozygosity rate</h3><p>Before genome assembly, genomic characteristics, including GC content, genome size, potential repeat content and heterozygosity rates, were estimated by ALLPATH-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. P. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513â1518 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2270">26</a></sup>. SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2274">42</a></sup> was adopted for assembly of the clean Illumina PE reads. K-mer sizes of 33, 43, 53 and 63 were examined using default parameters and the optimal k-mer size (kâ=â63) was selected based on the N50 length in each k-mer size. The resultant scaffolds were subjected to gap-filling with the Illumina PE reads by GapCloser 1.10 (Pâ=â31) (<a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/soapdenovo.html</a>) and scaffolds &gt;200âbp were selected and designated PCAB_r1.0.</p><p>To assess the degree of heterozygosity, sequencing errors, and/or potential assembly errors, we aligned all of the genome reads to the assembly using the BWA-MEM algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. arXiv 1303.3997 (2013)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2288">43</a></sup>. The depth of each base was calculated by the âdepthâ module of SAMTOOLS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2292">44</a></sup> with default settings. SNPs/InDels were called by the SAMTOOLS-BCFTOOLS pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2296">44</a></sup> (parameters: <i>samtools mpileup -q 1 -C 50 -g -t DP</i>, <i>SP -m 2</i>), followed by variants filtering steps implemented by vcfutils.pl (parameters: <i>vcfutils.pl varFilter -Q 20 -d 5 -D 250 -w 5 -W 10</i>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Repetitive sequence analysis</h3><p>The patchouli <i>de novo</i> repeat library was constructed using four complementary programs, LTR_finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2322">45</a></sup>, RepeatScout<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2326">46</a></sup>, PILER-DF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Edgar, R. C. &amp; Myers, E. W. PILER: identification and classification of genomic repeats. Bioinformatics 21, i152âi158 (2005)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2330">47</a></sup> and MITE-Hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199, 10.1093/nar/gkq862 (2010)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2334">48</a></sup>, followed by an additional classification step implemented by PASTEClassifier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Wicker, T. et al. A unified classification system for eukaryotic transposable elements. Nat. Rev. Genet. 8, 973â982 (2007)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2339">49</a></sup>. The resulting library sequences with their classification information were integrated with RepBase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2343">34</a></sup> to generate the final library, which was used to run RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Smit, A. F., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-3.0, &#xA;                    http://repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (1996â2010)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2347">50</a></sup> on the assembled scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>From the repeat-masked scaffolds &gt;1000âbp in PCAB_r1.0, genes were predicted by Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435âW439 (2006)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2359">32</a></sup> with a training set of <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR10). The parameters used were: speciesâ=âarabidopsis, genemodelâ=âpartial, proteinâ=âon, intronsâ=âon, startâ=âon, stopâ=âon, cdsâ=âon, codingseqâ=âon, alternatives-from-evidenceâ=âtrue, alternatives-from-samplingâ=âtrue, gff3â=âon, UTRâ=âon. The predicted genes were classified into four categories, intrinsic (with start and stop codons), partial (without start and/or stop codons), pseudo (with in-frame stop codons) and short genes (encoding &lt;50 amino acids).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Gene functional annotation</h3><p>Annotation of the predicted genes was performed by carrying out a BLAST analysis of patchouli sequences against a number of protein sequence databases, including the NCBI NR and COG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinformatics 4, 41 (2003)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2374">51</a></sup> databases, Swiss-Prot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Boeckmann, B. et al. The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 365â370 (2003)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2378">52</a></sup> and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Kanehisa, M., Goto, S., Sato, Y., Furumichi, M. &amp; Tanabe, M. KEGG for integration and interpretation of large-scale molecular data sets. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D109âD114 (2012)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2382">40</a></sup>, using an E-value cutoff of 1e-5. KEGG pathways were retrieved from the KEGG web server (<a href="http://www.genome.jp/kegg/">http://www.genome.jp/kegg/</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Kanehisa, M. et al. KEGG for linking genomes to life and the environment. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D480âD484 (2008)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2393">53</a></sup>. The output of the KEGG analysis includes KO assignments and KEGG pathways that are populated with the KO assignments. Domain-based alignments were carried out against the COG database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Tatusov, R. L. et al. The COG database: an updated version includes eukaryotes. BMC Bioinformatics 4, 41 (2003)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2398">51</a></sup> (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/COG/">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/COG/</a>) with a cut-off E-value of â¤1e-5. The resulting NR BLASTP hits were processed by the Blast2GO software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674â3676 (2005)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2409">35</a></sup> to retrieve associated GO terms with an E-value â¤1e-5 describing biological processes, molecular functions and cellular components<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2413">54</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Gene families identification</h3><p>The predicted proteins of <i>P. cablin</i> were pooled into a protein database with sequences &gt;50 amino acids from another four plant species, <i>S. lycopersicum</i> (Sol Genomics Network ITAG2.3 Release), <i>A. thaliana</i> (TAIR release 10), <i>S. indicum</i> and <i>M. guttatus</i>. For those having spliced variants, only the variants with the longest protein sequences were used. Pairwise sequence similarities between all protein sequences were calculated using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421, 10.1186/1471-2105-10-421 (2009)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2441">33</a></sup> with an E-value cutoff of 1e-05 and hits with identity &lt;30% and coverage &lt;30% were removed. Then, ORTHOMCL v2.0.9<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Fischer, S. et al. Using OrthoMCL to Assign Proteins to OrthoMCLâDB Groups or to Cluster Proteomes Into New Ortholog Groups. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics, 6.12. 11-16.12. 19 (2011)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2445">36</a></sup> was used to perform a Markov clustering algorithm to define the cluster structure, with a default inflation value (-I) of 1.5.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Phylogenetic tree reconstruction</h3><p>Single-copy gene families were retrieved from the ORTHOMCL results and used for the following phylogenetic maximum likelihood and Bayesian tree reconstruction steps. First, the proteins in each family were aligned using MUSCLE v3.8.31<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2457">55</a></sup> with default parameters. For Bayesian tree reconstruction, the CDS alignments of single-copy gene families were back-translated from the corresponding protein alignments and concatenated into a single supergene for each species using a custom Perl script. These supergenes were then subjected to phylogenetic analyses with MrBayes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Ronquist, F. &amp; Huelsenbeck, J. P. MrBayes 3: Bayesian phylogenetic inference under mixed models. Bioinformatics 19, 1572â1574 (2003)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2461">37</a></sup> using the GTR+Î+Î substitution model. For maximum likelihood tree reconstruction, the amino acid alignments of each gene family were assembled into supergenes and these supergenes were then subjected to MEGA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Tamura, K., Stecher, G., Peterson, D., Filipski, A. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA6: Molecular Evolutionary Genetics Analysis version 6.0. Mol Biol. Evol. 30, 2725â2729 (2013)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2465">56</a></sup> using the maximum likelihood method. The parameters used in the tree construction were the JTT model with gamma-distributed rates and 1000 bootstraps.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Divergence time estimation</h3><p>The CDS alignments of each gene family obtained in the Bayesian tree reconstruction step were separated into three partitions, corresponding to each one of the three codon positions in the CDS. The three supermatrices corresponding to each codon position were then separately assembled into âsupergenesâ using a custom Perl script. Divergence times were estimated under a relaxed clock model using the MCMCTree program in the PAML4.7 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2477">57</a></sup>. The âIndependent rates model (clockâ=â2)â and âJC69â model in MCMCTree were used in our calculation. The MCMC process is run for 4,000,000 iterations after a burn-in of 2,000,000 iterations. Other parameters used the default settings of MCMCTree. To check the robustness of the results, we ran the MCMCTree analysis twice and obtained similar results and a chronogram was produced using FigTree v1.4.0 (<a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/">http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/</a>) with the first run. We selected 120 and 130âMya as the lower and upper boundaries for the EurosidâAsterid split (Arabidopsisâtomato)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Hedges, S. B., Dudley, J. &amp; Kumar, S. TimeTree: a public knowledge-base of divergence times among organisms. Bioinformatics 22, 2971â2972 (2006)." href="/articles/srep26405#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d51722e2488">39</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec23" data-title="Additional Information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec23-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec23">Additional Information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec23-content"><p><b>How to cite this article</b>: He, Y. <i>et al.</i> Survey of the genome of <i>Pogostemon cablin</i> provides insights into its evolutionary history and sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis. <i>Sci. Rep.</i> <b>6</b>, 26405; doi: 10.1038/srep26405 (2016).</p></div></div></section>
